
# The Old Canoe

### 248 reprints from 1857-12-12 to 1899-08-25

## [The Old Canoe](https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/lccn/sn82014141/1860-03-01/ed-1/seq-1/)

#### From _St. Charles City Republican Intelligencer_

#### 1860-03-01

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

*THE OLD CANOE.**  
  
**BY EMILY R. PAGE.**  
  
Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is steep,    
And the waters below look dark and deep;    
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,    
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;    
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,    
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;    
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day thro&#x27;,    
Lies at its mooring, the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,    
Like a sea bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath lopped,    
And crossed on the railing one o&#x27;er one,    
Like folded hands when the work is done;    
While busily back and forth between,    
The spider stretches his silvery screen,    
And the solemn owl with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;    
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,    
Rots slowly away, in its living grave;    
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,    
Hiding the mouldering dust away,    
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,    
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;    
While many a blossom of loveliest hue    
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The current waters are dead and still,    
But the light winds play with the boat at will;    
And lazily in and out again,    
It floats the length of the rusty chain,    
Like the weary march of the hands of time,    
That meet and part at the noontide chime;    
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew    
By the dipping bow of the old canoe.  
  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,    
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,    
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,    
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,    
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,    
And looked below in the broken tide,    
To see that the faces and boats were two,    
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
  
But now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,    
And look below in the sluggish tide,    
The face that I see there is graver grown,    
And the laugh that I hear has a sobered tone,    
And the hand that lent to the light skiff wings    
Have grown familiar with sterner things;    
But I love to think of the hours that flew,    
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,    
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green moss grew,    
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
  
Bradford, Vt.
</td></tr></table>

## [From _The Union and eastern journal._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn83021380/1858-03-26/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1858-03-26 &middot; [Biddeford [Me.]](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Biddeford%2C_Maine)

rom the Home Journal.  
THE OLD CANOK.  
nY EMILY R. PAGE.  
In verse we have never seen anything more to  
the life than this. The moral, too, is a touching  
one. ED.  
Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the ragged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide:  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank­  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day thro&#x27;,  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath  
lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is doue;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery sereen,  
And the solemn owl, with a dull &quot;too hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave;  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still­  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runt  
quick-  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe!  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide.  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green moss grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

## [From _The flag of our Union._](https://archive.org/details/sim_flag-of-our-union_1858-08-07_13_32/page/n3/mode/1up?view=theater)

#### 1858-08-07 &middot; [Boston [Mass.]](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Boston)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
  
THE OLD CANOE,  
  
[The following sweet picture is from life, and though we  
published it nearly a year since, we again present it to  
our readers. taken from a London journal. We do not  
wonder, as we read the musical versification, and enjoy  
the delicate and yet vivid truthfulness of the poem,  
that it shonld have heen so extensively copied in Euro-  
pean journals, after going the extended round of the  
American press. It is from the pen of our favorite  
contributor, Miss Emmy R. Pas.)  
  
Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank—  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lays at its moorings the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
  
Like a sea-bird’s wings that the storm hath lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
  
While busily back and forth between,  
  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
  
And the solemn owl, with his dull ‘+ too hoo,”  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
  
O, many a time, with a careless hand,  
  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick—  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick—  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe!  
  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling sido,  
  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green moss grew  
  
O’er the moulderiug stern of the old canoe.

## [From _New Hampshire journal of agriculture._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn90062042/1859-06-09/ed-1/?sp=4)

#### 1859-06-09 &middot; [Manchester, N.H.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Manchester%2C_New_Hampshire)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

is still unmarried, and still forty-eight.  
LALE.  
  
From the Flag of our Union.  
The Cld Canoe.  
BY EMILY R. PAGE.  
Where the rocks are gray; and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep ;  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide ;  
‘Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank—  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lays at its morings the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird’s wings that the storm hath lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done ;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull * too hoo*”?  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower ;  
‘While many a blossom of loveliest hue y  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the moontide chime ; .  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
  
0, many a time, with a careless hand,  
. I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick—  
‘Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick—  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrowed back from the old canoe !  
  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with ‘sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green moss grew  
Q’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
  
Bad

## [From _The liberator._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84031524/1859-10-21/ed-1/?sp=4)

#### 1859-10-21 &middot; [Boston, Mass.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Boston)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

.  
BY MISS EMILY B. PAGE.  
Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide ;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank ;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through—  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird’s wing that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing one o’er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn ow], with his dull ‘ too-hoo,’  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower ;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o’er the stern of old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick—  
Where the whirls are wild, and the eddies are thick—  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
O’er the mouldering stem of the old canoe.  
THE BORE O
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://tile.loc.gov/image-services/iiif/service:ndnp:mb:batch_mb_selene_ver01:data:sn84031524:print:1859102101:6034/pct:5.548504,8.256518,14.723481,31.129839/!600,600/0/default.jpg"/>

The OlD Canee.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
Loans gloomily over the murky tide:  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lays at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles areidly dropped,  
Like a sea bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath lepped,  
And crosed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Lke folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too hoo,  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay.  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
It fleats the length of its rusty chain.  
Ltke the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canos.  
O, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the breken tide,  
To see that tho faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrered back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the slugglish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings, I  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
But I love to think of the hours that fiew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray threw,  
Are the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the siouldering stern of the old canoe.

THE OLD CANOE.  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is  
steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deept  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,

bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
thro&#x27;,

The useless paddies are idly dropped,  
Like a seasbird&#x27;s wings that the wind hath  
lop&#x27;d,  
And crossed on the railing one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen;  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,

T,

That meet and part at the noontide chime;

quick

thick

wings,

threw,

## [From _Fremont journal._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn85026050/1859-12-23/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1859-12-23 &middot; [Fremont, Sandusky County [Ohio]](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Fremont%2C_Ohio)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

 PRoPRIeTOr.  
  
TEAE OLD CADOaE.  
  
KANSAS STATE GARETTE.  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lays at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solenin owl with his dull &quot;too hoo  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the toomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of lively hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still­  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pulled it away from the pebbly strand,  
Anfi paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and the boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the moldering stern of the old canoe.  
  
Rober
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://tile.loc.gov/image-services/iiif/service:ndnp:ohi:batch_ohi_cobweb_ver04:data:sn85026050:00280775848:1859122301:0213/pct:17.901578,14.120832,23.974002,81.661442/!600,600/0/default.jpg"/>

## [From _Richmond enquirer._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84024735/1859-12-26/ed-1/?sp=4)

#### 1859-12-26 &middot; [Richmond, Va.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Richmond%2C_Virginia)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

PoETICAL.  
THE OLD CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steer,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide,  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
through,  
Lays at its mooring the old cance.  
The uscless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath  
lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done,  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider Stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too hoe,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old cance.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave.  
And the green mnoss crceps ofer its dull decay,  
Iiding the mouldering duist away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the tallen tower;  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue  
Springs up ofer the stern of the old cance,  
The currentless waters are dead and still  
Bot the light winds play with the boat at will,  
  
And lazily in and out again,  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the nioontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time with a careldss hand  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick-  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the breken tide,  
To see that the faces of the boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side;  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray  
threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of tise old canoe.

 The OLd cAnOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, in its lone&#x27;y pride,

hile busily back and forth between,

qulek-

thick

## [From _The Nevada Democrat._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84026881/1860-01-25/ed-1/?sp=4)

#### 1860-01-25 &middot; [Nevada, Calif.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Nevada_City%2C_California)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

THE OLD CANOE.  
  
Where the rocks arc gray and tka abort la steep,  
And the water* below look dark and deep,  
Wbei e the rugged pine, in it* lonely pride,  
Lean* gloomily oeer the murky tide ;  
Where the reed* and runhe* are long and rank,  
And the wee da grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the thadow ia heary the whole day through,  
Lite at it* mooring* the old canoe.  
The ntelesa paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a tea-bird’* wing* that the storm hat lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, on* o’er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work la done;  
While buiaily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches hit silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull “too-hoo,”  
Settles down on the side of the old saaoe.  
The stern hall stink in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in Its living grave,  
And the green moss creep* o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that piaote * &#x27;re the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivey that mantle* the tailing tower;  
While many a blosom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
it floats the length of its rusty chain,  
like the weary march of the hand of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebly strand ;  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick—  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick—  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
And louked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the face* and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old cone*.  
Rut now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there I* graver grown,  
And the laugh that I bear has a sober tons,  
And the hands that lent to the light skilf wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things:  
But I love to think of the hours that Hew  
As 2 rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,  
Ire the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
U’er the mouldering stern ot the old canoe.

## [From _The Buchanan County guardian._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn87058348/1860-02-09/ed-1/?sp=1)

## [From _St. Charles City Republican intelligencer._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn82014141/1860-03-01/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1860-03-01 &middot; [St. Charles City [Charles City], Iowa](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Charles_City%2C_Iowa)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
THE OLD CANOE.  
  
Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day thro&#x27;,  
Lies at its moorings, the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath lopped,  
And crossed on the railing one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly a way, in its living grave  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb.  
  
a flower,  
  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light winds play with the boat at will;  
And lazily in and out again,  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
By the dipping bow of the old canoe.  
  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,  
  
And laughod as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
  
But now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a sobered tone,  
And the hand that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green moss grew,  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
Bradford, Vt.
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://tile.loc.gov/image-services/iiif/service:ndnp:iahi:batch_iahi_charizard_ver01:data:sn82014141:00279529455:1860030101:0671/pct:75.306562,29.612871,9.604044,23.301234/!600,600/0/default.jpg"/>
</td>
</tr></table>

## [From _St. Cloud Democrat._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn83016836/1860-03-22/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1860-03-22 &middot; [Saint Cloud, Stearns County, Minn.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/St._Cloud%2C_Minnesota)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

H.  
  
E O A N O E  
  
The following beautiful lines have been going  
the rounds of newspaperdom for several years  
without credit. Whoever the author is. and  
we confess our inability to name him, he should  
claim the wandering progeny of bis brain, as  
they are worthy of the palmiest days of genuine  
poetry:  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky side  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank  
Where shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lays at its moorings the old cause.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-birds&#x27;swings that thestormhathlop&#x27;  
And crossed on the railing one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silver screen  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-whoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
currentless waters are dead and still  
But the light winds play with the boat at will  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime  
And the shore is kissed at each time anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
  
many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pulled it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddl&#x27;d it down where the stream runs quick  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies thick.  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
  
But now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear Las a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
Butllovetothinkof the hours that flew, [threw,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray  
I Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew,  
  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

## [From _Holmes County farmer._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84028822/1860-07-12/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1860-07-12 &middot; [Millersburg, Ohio](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Millersburg%2C_Ohio)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  e  
  
THE OLD CANOE.  
  
e e e  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomingly over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and the rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wing that the wind hath lop&#x27;d,  
And crossed on the railing one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done:  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its full decay,  
Hiding its smouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue,  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe,  
O, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pulled it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and forms were two  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide.  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
But I love to think of the hours that flew, Ithrew,  
As I rock&#x27;d where the whirls their white spray  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
  
  
  
SP
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://tile.loc.gov/image-services/iiif/service:ndnp:ohi:batch_ohi_edgar_ver01:data:sn84028822:00280775459:1860071201:0094/pct:5.476369,13.012353,26.781695,27.695115/!600,600/0/default.jpg"/>
</td>

## [From _Holmes County farmer._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84028822/1861-09-26/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1861-09-26 &middot; [Millersburg, Ohio](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Millersburg%2C_Ohio)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

 and for sale lwo  
WEIMER A STEINEAOHLR.  
  
Dackrp.  
  
THE OLD CANOE.  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through  
Lays at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath lopped.  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen;  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue,  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light winds play with the boat at w  
And laziy in and out again,  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pulled it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick  
And I laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And looked below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the skiff light wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray  
threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

## [From _Holmes County farmer._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84028822/1863-04-23/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1863-04-23 &middot; [Millersburg, Ohio](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Millersburg%2C_Ohio)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

:  
  
THE OLD CANOE.  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide  
When the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thich on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day thro&#x27;,  
Lays at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl with his dull &quot;too hoo,&quot;  
Settles on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the moldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the toomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
  
Springs up o&#x27;r the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in end out again,  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pulled it away from the pebly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone,  
And the hand that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
But I love to talk of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirl their wild spray  
threw,  
O&#x27;er the moldering stern of th
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://tile.loc.gov/image-services/iiif/service:ndnp:ohi:batch_ohi_edgar_ver01:data:sn84028822:00280775459:1863042301:0696/pct:18.555189,15.406400,12.467913,24.695554/!600,600/0/default.jpg"/>

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

,  
  
thn oud Gan  
  
Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is steep,  
And the water below looked dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide  
When the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank  
here the shadow is heavy the whole day through  
Lay at its moorings the old canoe,  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s winge that the storm hath lopped,  
And crossed on the raising, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work ia done;  
While buisily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemu owl, with his dull &quot;too hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canpe,  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creaps o&#x27;er its dull decay  
Hiding the moldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants ofer the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the storn of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are doad and still  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pulled it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stre m runs quick;  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see if the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe,  
But now as I lean ofer the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide  
The face that I see there is grayer grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familier with sterner things,  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the greon grass grew,  
G&#x27;er the moldering stern of the old canoe.

The Old Canoe.  
  
Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is

## [From _The weekly union record._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn86076422/1865-07-08/ed-1/?sp=4)

#### 1865-07-08 &middot; [Oroville, Calif.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Oroville%2C_California)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

.  
  
THE OLD CAHOE.  
  
Wbete the rock* are pray and the slope U sleep.  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the ragged pine in its lonely pride.  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reek and rushes are tall and rank.  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow la heavy the whole day through.  
Lay at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped.  
Like a seabird&#x27;s wings that the storm had lopped,  
And crossed on the railings, one o’er one.  
Like folded hands when the work ia done:  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches her silver screen.  
And the solemn owl, with his dull ‘too-hoo,”  
Settles down oa the side of the old canoe.  
0. many a time, when a careless hand,  
I hare pushed it away from the pebbly strand.  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirles are wild and the eddies are thick.  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boat* were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown.  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I luve to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray threw.  
Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old cauoe.

## [From _Bradford reporter._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84024558/1865-08-10/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1865-08-10 &middot; [Towanda, Pa.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Towanda%2C_Pennsylvania)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
THE OLD CANOE.  
We take great pleasure in reproducing this ex­  
quisite poem, cut many years ago from an obscure  
Arkansas paper, and published in the North.—  
Every now and then it re-appears on the surface of  
newspaper literature. It desires a more prominent  
place in our letters :  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
&#x27; And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide ;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank ;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The uselesss paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done ;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tonib a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower ;  
While many a blossom of the loveliest hue,  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waves are dead and still—  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the wenry march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime.  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew.  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand ;  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
And now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skill&#x27; wings,  
Have grown familial- with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that flew  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew,  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stem of the old canoe.  
MOUN
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://tile.loc.gov/image-services/iiif/service:ndnp:pst:batch_pst_jeffrey_ver01:data:sn84024558:00296028654:1865081001:0325/pct:55.335025,14.863667,13.192061,32.254489/!600,600/0/default.jpg"/>
</td>
</tr></table>

## [From _The West Virginia journal._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84026789/1865-09-20/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1865-09-20 &middot; [Charleston, W. Va.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Charleston%2C_West_Virginia)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

.  
THE OLD CANOE.  
We take great pleasuge in reproducing this exquisite  
poem, which appeared many years ago in an ulincure  
Arkantsas paper. Every now and then it appears on the  
surface of newspaper literature. It deserves a more per.­  
mantent place in our letters.  
Where the rocks are grey and the shore is  
steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding  
bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
through,  
Lies at its meorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea bird&#x27;s wing that the storm has  
loppe!.  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one.  
like the folled hamds when the work is lone:  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider Stretches his silvery sereen.  
And the solemn owl, with his dull&quot;too-hoo.&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rtots slowly away in its living grave,  
An! the grcen mosst crocps o&#x27;er its dall decay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
lake the hands that plant o&#x27;er the tomb a  
llower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue,  
See wo oer the ston of the eld came  
The currentless waters are dead and still-  
Eat the liaht wirnd plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
Aed the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time, with a carcless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly sand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick,  
And laughed as I leaned ofer the rocking side  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old cance.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone.  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff  
wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
But I love to think of the hours that flew  
Ass I rocked where the whirls their white  
spray threw,  
Ere the blossom waved, or the green grass  
grew  
Oer the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
  
Stern Wheel Pre

## [From _Bedford inquirer._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn83032006/1866-12-21/ed-1/?sp=1)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

.  
THE OLD CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is  
steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide ;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank  
And the weeds grow thick on thewindingbank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
through  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has  
looped.  
And crossed on the railing one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily hack and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with its dull &#x27;&quot;too-too,  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull de.  
cay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a  
flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower ;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue.  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stem of the old canoe.  
The currentles3 waves are dead and still-  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meets and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
j3y the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
  
0, many a time, with a careless hand.  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand;  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick,  
And laughed as 1 leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old cauoe.  
  
And now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that flew  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
  
Ere the blossom waved, or the green grass  
  
grew.

The Old Canoe-  
  
Where the rocks are gray and shore  
is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and  
deep,

pride

hue

is quick,  
Where the whirls are wild ftnd the.

THE OLD CANOE.

And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank.  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
lies at its moorings the old *  
canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped, ,  
Like a sea-birds wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one.  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
gee.-- ,9&quot;V vault -ite lUitu ++*&quot;‘YY&quot;  
The spider stretches his silvery screen.  
Ana the solemn owl, with his dull &quot; toc  
-.... -_ too-hoe,&quot;  
Nestles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly awnv in its living grave.  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away.  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles a fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of livliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light winds play with the boat at will.  
And lazily in and out again  
it floats the length of its rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;

And the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pushed it hway from the pebbly strand.  
And paddled it down where the stream ran quick—  
■Where the whirls were wild and the storm was thick;  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boam were two  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below m the sluggish tide  
The face that I see is graver grown.  
And the laugh that I heard has a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to &gt; he light skiff winge  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray threw;  
Ere the blossoms moved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

## [From _Staunton spectator._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84024718/1867-06-25/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1867-06-25 &middot; [Staunton, Va.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Staunton%2C_Virginia)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

. .  
The Old Canoe.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep :  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide ;  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day thro&#x27;  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Nestles down on the side of the old can«e.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the moldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles a fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern ofthe old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream ran quick,  
Where the whirls were wild and the storm was  
thick;  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the brokeji tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide  
The face that I see is graver grown,  
And the laugh I heard has a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things ;  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray  
threw;  
Ere the blossoms moved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the moldering stern ofthe old canoe.

## [From _The sun._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn83030272/1867-07-23/ed-1/?sp=4)

#### 1867-07-23 &middot; [New York [N.Y.]](http://dbpedia.org/resource/New_York_City)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe,  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-birds wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;toob  
Nestles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Iots slowly in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Il ling the mouldering dust away.  
Lke the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that maniles a fallen tower;  
While many a blssom of liveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old cancoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of its rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn ancw  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand.  
And piddled it down where the streain ran quick,  
Where the whirls were wild and the storm was  
thick;  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the slurgish tide,  
The face that I see is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I heard has a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff win  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love so think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray  
threw,  
Ere the blossoms moyed or the green grass gre  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

And laughed, as I leaned on the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe,  
But now, as I lean on the crumbling side

As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray  
threw,

## [From _Walla Walla statesman._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn86072040/1867-11-08/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1867-11-08 &middot; [Walla Walla, Wash. Terr.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Walla_Walla%2C_Washington)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

 ANd THE URIOE.  
  
wher the reTnn onn cano.  
re the rocks are gray and the shore is steep  
And the waters below lbok ark and desp   
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.Ithrough,  
The useless paddies are idly dropped,  
Like a seasbird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one, &quot;  
Like folded hands when the work is done,  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Nestles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slotyn its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the moldering dust away.  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles a fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hand of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream ran quick,  
Where the whirls were wild and the storm was  
  
thick!  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I heard has a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
Bat I love to think of the hours that flew, Ithrew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray  
&#x27;Ere the blossoms moved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the moldering stern of the old canoe.

The Old Canoe. : -  
j  
Whore the- rocks are gray and the shore-is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,.  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide j  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lies at the moorings the old canoe.  
The useless Daddies are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done ;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches bis silvery screen ;  
And the solemn owl, with the dull &quot;Bo-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the sides of the old canoe.

The Old Canoe.

*“d the waters below look dark and deep,  
nhere the ragged nine in its lonely pride  
beans gloomily over the murky tide;  
n here the reeds ami rushes are tall and rank,  
here the reeus grow thick on the winding hank,  
here the shadow is heavy the whole day through.  
Lies at its moorings the old yinoc.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea bird s wings that the storm hath stopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one.  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
&quot;“lie busily back und forth between,  
The spider stretches his silver screen ;  
And the solemn owl with his dull “too whoo,”  
Nestles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its hidden grave :  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering past away.  
Like the hand that plauts o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the Ivy that mantles a falling tower;  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waves are dead and still.  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of its rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet ami part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
By the dipping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand  
And paddled it down where the stream ran quick.  
When the whirls were wild ami the storm was thick ;  
And laughed as I leaned on the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide.  
To see that the faces an&#x27;d boats were two,  
That rye re mirretk-d back from the old canoe.  
But now. as I lean on the crumbling side  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
Tihe face that I see Is graver grown.  
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone.  
And the ham! that leant to the light skiff wings,  
Has grown familiar with sterner things;  
But 1 love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray flew,  
Kre the blossoms moved or the green grass grew  
O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

## [From _The new North-west._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84038125/1870-03-04/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1870-03-04 &middot; [Deer Lodge, Mont.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Deer_Lodge%2C_Montana)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

.  
THE OLD CANOO.  
The following beautiful poem appeared some years  
ago in an obscure Arkansas paper:  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep.  
And the water below looks dark and deep.  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lics at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddies are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the ralling one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screent,  
And the solemn owl with his dull too-hoo.&quot;  
Scttles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave;  
And the grees moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust away  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and stil­  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That mect and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kiesed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddied it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And tooked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
Bnt now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side  
And look below in the singgish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown.  
And the laugh that I hear bas a soberer tone;  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Mave grown famillar with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that dew  
As I rock&#x27;d where th&#x27; whirls their light apray threw.  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

## [From _The Columbian._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn83032011/1870-04-01/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1870-04-01 &middot; [Bloomsburg, Pa.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Bloomsburg%2C_Pennsylvania)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
The Old Canoe.  
The following beautiful poem appeared some  
years ago in an obscure Arkansas paper:  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride  
eans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
through,  
les at its moorings the old canoe,  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
ike a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lop-  
ped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
tke the folded hands when the work is done:  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-whoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
tot slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
tiing its mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
or the ivy that manties the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe,  
The currentless waters are dead and still  
And the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
and the shore is Kissed af each turning anew,  
by the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time, with a careless hand.  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand;  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
nd the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
But I love to think of the hours that flew  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
threw,  
Ere the blossom waved, or the green grass grew,  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
Sherida
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://tile.loc.gov/image-services/iiif/service:ndnp:pst:batch_pst_carnegie_ver01:data:sn83032011:00237287976:1870040101:0400/pct:35.188715,15.272470,11.685093,26.411890/!600,600/0/default.jpg"/>

</tr></table>

## [From _Sunbury American._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84026403/1870-04-23/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1870-04-23 &middot; [Sunbury, Pa.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Sunbury%2C_Pennsylvania)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through  
Lies at its moorings the old canoc.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped  
And crossed on the railing one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery sereen  
And the solemn owl with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimmy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay  
Hiding its moldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower  
While many a blossom of lovliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoc.  
The currentless waters are dead and still  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the streams runs  
quiok,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that flew  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
WHER

## The Old Canoe.

#### From _Christian Advocate (1866-1905)_

#### 1873-08-21 &middot; [Chicago](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Chicago)

[This text is not available under an open license.]

## [From _Independent statesman._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84022957/1873-08-21/ed-1/?sp=7)

#### 1873-08-21 &middot; [Concord, N.H.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Concord%2C_New_Hampshire)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

Young Folks.  
* THE OLD CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugzed pine in its lonely pride,  
| Leaus gloomily over the murky tide;  
I Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
| And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
| Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
| There lies at its mooring the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird’s wing that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery sereen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull “too-hoo,”  
| Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
| The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss ereeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of lovliest hue  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
1t floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old caroe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
Ihave pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick  
And lauihed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laughjthat I hear has a soberer tone;  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As 1 x}&#x27;lo(&#x27;;]ked where the whirls their white spray  
shed,  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass grew,  
O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
THAT BO

## [From _Staunton spectator._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84024718/1873-08-26/ed-1/?sp=1)

SDAY, AUGUST 26, 1873.  
  
  
.Yoetry.  
The Old Canoe.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep.  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomly over the murky tide ;  
Where the reads and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
through,  
There lies at its moorings the old.canoe.  
The useless piddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopp­  
ed,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower ;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore i 3 kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,  
Where tho whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces end boats were two,  
That were mirrored b.ick from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that 1 hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wiugs  
Have grown familiar with sterner things ;  
But 1 love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
shed,  
Ere the blossom waved, or the green grass grew,  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.
