ROMANCE U POLITICS.  
The Columbus State Journal tells  
the story of love of some Ohio poli­  
ticians, among others, of Hon. William  
Allen, the Democratic nominee for the  
Governorship of the State. In 1830  
Mr. Allen was nominated for Congress  
by the Democrats, and Mr. McArthur,  
the Whig Governor, was a candidate f er  
re-ekcticn. Mr. Allen loved the Gov­  
ernor&#x27;s daughter, but the father broke  
off the match, and the lady married a  
Kentuckian, who died before the end of  
Mr. Allen&#x27;s term in the House. Gov­  
ernor McArthur nlso died. Now comes  
what seems almost the action of a deus  
ex machina. There was anultra-Democrat  
whose daughter loved and wedded  
a Whig, when the father had had his  
eye upon Allen G. Thurman as an eligi­  
ble husband for her. Soon he, too, slept  
with his fathers, the lady became a  
widow and married Mr. Thurman. At  
the wedding Mr. Allen again met Miss  
McArthur (now a widow). The old love  
returned, and they, too, begame hus­  
band and wife. Here is plenty ofre-man-e  
for a dime novel.  
&quot;But stop, mychild.and listen to me.

=  
  
TIM OLD CANOE  
  
[The following fine verses appeared annony­  
mously saran years ago in a paper at Little  
Rock, Arkansas. The writer could have well  
afforded to giro his or her name with it.]  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,

## [From _The Somerset herald._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84026409/1873-09-03/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1873-09-03 &middot; [Somerset, Pa.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Somerset%2C_Pennsylvania)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

.  
  
THE OLD CANOE.  
 eeeeeee eae  
The following fine verses appeared  
anonymously some years ago in a pa-  
per at Little Rock, Arkansas. The  
writter could have well afforded to  
give his or her name with it.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide:  
Where the reeds and the rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done:  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silver screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of lovilest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoc.  
The currentless waters are dead and still  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below at the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wing  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray shed,  
Ere the blossom waved, or the green grass grew,  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
MY BACHELO
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://tile.loc.gov/image-services/iiif/service:ndnp:pst:batch_pst_grange_ver01:data:sn84026409:00280776464:1873090301:0264/pct:31.500833,13.730159,10.737544,25.249433/!600,600/0/default.jpg"/>

</tr></table>

ROMANCE IN POLITICS.  
The Columbus State Journal tells |  
the story of love of some Ohio poli- &lt;  
tician3, among others, of Hon. William 1  
All&lt;:n, the Democratic nominee for the 1  
Governorship of the State. In 1830 j  
Mr. Allen was nominated for Congress j  
by the Democrats, and Mr. McArthur, &lt;  
the Whig Governor, was a candidate for  
rc-electicn. Mr. Allen loved the Gov- 1  
  
ernor s daughter, but the father broke  
off the match, and the lady married a  
Iventuckian, who died before the end of  
Mr. Allen&#x27;s term in the House. Governor  
McArthur also died. Now comes  
what seems almost the action of a dcus  
cx machina. Thero was an ultra-Democrat  
whose daughter loved and wedded  
a Whig, when the father had had his  
eye upon Allen G. Thurman as an eligible  
husband for her. Soon he, too, slept  
with his fathers, the lqdy became a  
widow and married Mr. Thurman. At  
tho wedding Mr. Allen again met Miss  
McArthur (now a widow). The old love  
returned, and they, too, became husband  
and wife. Here is plenty of romance  
for a dime novel.

THE OLD CANOE.  
Sn a :  
HERE the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the rugged pine, in its lonely pride.  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
There lies at its mooring the old ‘canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, onc o&#x27;er one,  
  
Like the folded hands when the work is done:  
While busily back ana forth between  
  
The spider watches his silver screen,  
  
And the solemn owl, with his dull ‘* too-hoo,”  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave.  
  
Rots slowly away in its living grave  
  
And the green mass creeps o’er its dull decay:  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tome’a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower:  
While many a blossom of lovelest hue  
  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light winds play with the boat at will.  
And lazily in and out again  
  
It fioats the length of the rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
  
Oh, many a time, with a careless’ hand,  
  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs qnick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick.  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side.

To see that the faces and boats were two,  
  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.

And look below in the sluggish tide,

## [From _The Holt County sentinel._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn85034039/1873-09-12/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1873-09-12 &middot; [Oregon, Missouri.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Oregon%2C_Missouri)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
HE OLD CANOE.  
te thtwatete below loox dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky fide;  
ere the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thiak ou the winting hant  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Ldke a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Liike the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silver screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
otic tioh on the sie of the oi Caniou.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower  
1 suny a blossoi of foveliest nu  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have puished it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
Hut now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
shed,  
Ere the blossom waved, or the green grass grew,  
O&#x27;er the moldering stern of the old canoe.  
MISCELLANY.
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">

## [From _Dodgeville chronicle._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn85033019/1873-09-19/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1873-09-19 &middot; [Dodgeville, Wis.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Dodgeville%2C_Wisconsin)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
VOLUME XII.  
  
THE OLD CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And (he waters below iook dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a seabird’s wings that the storm&#x27; has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
Like the f lded hands when the work is done;  
While busily hack and forth.between,  
The spiflcr stretches hie silvery • reen,  
And thsofainni&gt;h4; with his dull ’•■too-boo,”  
Kettles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Hots slowly away in its livitig grave,  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding 1 s moldering dust away—  
Like the hand that, plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest lme  
Springs up o’er the tjteru of the old oanoe.  
The currenllcss waters are dead and still—  
lint the light wind pluys wiih the lioat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
Jt floats the length, of the-rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That mOel and part at the noon-tide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
Isy the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
i have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side—  
And looked below in the broken tide—  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
lint now, ns I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
Tin: face that i see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skill wrings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that sped,  
As 1 rocked where the whirls their white spray  
shed.  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
O’er the moldering stern of the old canoe.  
THE DESERT ISLAND.

## [From _Wood County reporter._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn85033078/1873-10-02/ed-1/?sp=2)

#### 1873-10-02 &middot; [Grand Rapids [i.e. Wisconsin Rapids], Wis.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Wisconsin_Rapids%2C_Wisconsin)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

ocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
AnfTthe waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride  
Leans gloomily over (he murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank.  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a seabird’s wings that the storm has lopped.  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one.  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between.  
The spider stretches hie silvery screen.  
And the solemn owl, with his dull “too-hoo,”  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave.  
Rots slowly away in its living grave.  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hidimr its moldering dust away—  
Like idle hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentloss waters are dead and still—  
Bur the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again.  
It floats the length of the rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time.  
That meet and part at the noon tide chime.  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oli. many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebblv strand.  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick.  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick.  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side—  
And looked below in the broken tide—  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side.  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that 1 see there is graver grown.  
And the laugh that 1 hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner ihiugs;  
But I love to think of the hours that sped.  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
shed.  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
O’er the moldering stern of the old canoe.

## [From _The People._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84020392/1873-10-16/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1873-10-16 &middot; [Concord, N.H.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Concord%2C_New_Hampshire)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

.  
  
Poetry.  
*  
THOE OLD CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rufged pine, 1 its lonely pride, .  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
There lies at its moorings an old canoe.  
The useless paddlcs are idly dropped,  
Like a sca-bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
Like the folded hands when the w ork is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull “toe-hoe,”  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss cre%s o’er its dull decay,  
llidiui its mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Spring up o’er the stern of the ¢ld canoe.  
The currentiess waters are dead and still—  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazilg in and out again  
It floats the length of the rustljlr chain,  
Like the weax(&#x27;iy march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
1 have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are \y&amp;ld and&#x27;the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I&#x27;leanéd o’er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the brokén tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver %rown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray shed  
Ere the blossom waved, or the green grass grew,  
G’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe,  
R ——  
A SONG FOR THE HARVEST.  
B

Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,

elke a sea-bird&#x27;s wing that the storm has lopred,

That were mirrored back from the old canoe.

## [From _Staunton vindicator._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84024653/1874-04-24/ed-1/?sp=4)

#### 1874-04-24 &middot; [Staunton, Va.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Staunton%2C_Virginia)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

.  
THE OLD CANOE  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the nigged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over tlif murky tide ;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank.  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank ;  
Where the shadow is heavy itlie whole day through,  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
Like the lolded hands when the work is done ;  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull “too-lioo,”  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And tlie green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower :  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springing up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
&#x27;phe currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the lengths of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick­  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggislj tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown.  
And the laugh that I hear lias a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray shed,  
Ere the blossom waved, or the green grass grew,  
O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
H U MO R.

THE-OLD CANOE.  
■Where the rocka are gray and the shore is steep.  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride  
.Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the roods and rushes are long and rank.  
And the weds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through.  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped.  
Xdke a sea-bird’s wings that the storm has lopped.  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one.  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery son •.i.  
And the solemn owl with hisj dull *• to &gt; ,  
Settles down on the aide of the old canoe. ,  
The stern, half-sunk, in the slimy wave,  
Rota slowly away in its living grave.  
And the green moss creeps over its dull decay.  
Hiding its moldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb of a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentloss waters are dead and still;  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out ag in  
It floats the length of tne rusty chain,  
Like the weary march ox the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew.  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Ob. many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly Strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick.  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick.  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side.  
And looked below in the broken tide.  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old oanoe.  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide.  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that! hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hand that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to thimc of the hours that sped

through

## The Old Canoe

## [From _Fayette County herald._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn85038145/1876-01-20/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1876-01-20 &middot; [Washington [Washington Court House], Fayette County, O. [Ohio]](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Washington_Court_House%2C_Ohio)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore Is stcop&#x27;  
And the waters below&#x27;look dark and deep, :  
  
Where the rugged pine In his lofty pride  
  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
  
Where the reeds and rashes are long and rank.  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank ;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day thro,&#x27;  
  
There lies at Its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped, -  
  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lop­  
ped.And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one  
Like the foldi.d hands, when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
  
The spider stretches his silvery screen.  
  
And the solemn owl with his dull too-woo.&quot;  
  
Settles down on the side ef the old canoe.  
The stern, half-sank, in the slimy wave.  
  
Hots slowly away in its living grave, ;  
And the green moss creeps over Its doll decay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust away, &#x27; &quot; &#x27; ,  
  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er a tomb a flower.  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower ;  
  
While many a blossom of lovktest hue  
Springs np o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe. .  
The currentless waters are dead and still;  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will. .  
And lazily In and out again . ..  
It floats the length of the rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
  
That meet and part at the nonetide chime.  
  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew.  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless band.  
I have pushed it way from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick.  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide.  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
Cut, now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side.  
And look below in the sluggish tide.  
The face that I sec there is graver grown.  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone.  
And the hands that lent to the light skiffs wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things .  
But 1 love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
shed,  
Ire the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the moldering stern of the old canoe.  
  
THE WIDOW BARLOW.  
  
BY MARY

## The Old Cange

#### From _St. Louis globe-Democrat._

#### 1876-03-19 &middot; [St. Louis [Mo.]](http://dbpedia.org/resource/St._Louis)

[This text is not available under an open license.]

---

## [From _The Albion, A Journal of News, Politics and Literature (1822-1876)_](https://archive.org/details/sim_albion-a-journal-of-news-politics-and-literature_1876-04-01_54_14/page/n3/mode/1up?view=theater)

#### 1876-04-01 &middot; [New York](http://dbpedia.org/resource/New_York_City)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
  
The Old Canoe.  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And tke waters below look dark and deep,  
  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,’  
  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
  
Like a sea bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
  
And the solemn owl with the dull “too whoo,”,  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe,  
  
The currentless waters are dead and atill,  
  
But the twilight wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
  
Oh, many a time with ceaseless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick, —  
  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
‘That were mirrored back from the old canoe,  
  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
  
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,  
  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
shed  
  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass grew  
  
O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  

</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://iiif.archive.org/image/iiif/2/sim_albion-a-journal-of-news-politics-and-literature_1876-04-01_54_14%2Fsim_albion-a-journal-of-news-politics-and-literature_1876-04-01_54_14_jp2.zip%2Fsim_albion-a-journal-of-news-politics-and-literature_1876-04-01_54_14_jp2%2Fsim_albion-a-journal-of-news-politics-and-literature_1876-04-01_54_14_0003.jp2/pct:7.001134,11.719911,20.068027,37.314264/,600/0/default.jpg"/>
</td>
</tr></table>

sleep,

bank;

through,

lopped,

And the solemn owl with his dull “to-who.”

But the light wind plays with the boat at will.

Aud paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,

## [From _Bristol news._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn85026955/1876-04-11/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1876-04-11 &middot; [Bristol, Va. & Tenn.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Bristol%2C_Virginia)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

.  
ALVALANCHE,  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore steep  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through;  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a seabird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with the dull &quot;too-whoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe,  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly in its living grave;  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er his dull decay  
Hiding its moulding dust away,  
Like the hands that plants o&#x27;er a tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the twilight wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
It floats the length of its rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part the noonday chime.  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew.  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe,  
Oh, many a time with ceaseless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wide and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrowed back from the old canoe.  
But now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is grave grown,  
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their whites pray shed  
Ere the blossom or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe

## [From _Rocky Mountain husbandman._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn83025309/1876-06-08/ed-1/?sp=5)

#### 1876-06-08 &middot; [Diamond City, Mont.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Great_Falls%2C_Montana)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

.  
Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a seabird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl with the dull &quot; too-whoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave;  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er his dull decay  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er a tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of lovliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the twilight wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noonday chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time with ceaseless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wide and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
shed,  
Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
[ Written fo

## [From _Bradford opinion._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn84022553/1876-12-30/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1876-12-30 &middot; [Bradford, Vt.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Bradford%2C_Vermont)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

, Proprigior.  
  
The Old Cance.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the reeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
  
through,  
has  
There lies at its moorings the old cance.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea bird&#x27;s wings that the storm  
lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done,  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider strotches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl with his dull &quot;to-who,&quot;  
Settles down on the sides of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps over its dull decay,  
Hiding its moldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a  
flower,  
Of the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the sides of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
from the pebbly strand  
where the stream runs  
I have pushed it away  
And paddled it down w  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
shed,  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the moldering stern of the old canoe

## [From _The Woman's Journal 1870-1916_](https://archive.org/details/sim_the-womans-journal_1879-04-05_10_14/page/n1/mode/1up?view=theater)

#### 1879-04-05 &middot; [Boston, Massachusetts](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Boston)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

.  
  
THE OLD CANOE.  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride  
  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide:  
  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
  
Like a seabird’s wings that the storm hath lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
  
And the solemn owl, with his dull ‘‘too hoo,”  
Nestles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots elowly away in its living grave,  
  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the moldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that plauts o’er the tomb a flower,  
Of the ivy that mantles a fallen tower;  
  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue  
  
Springs up o’er the bow of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the bands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream ran quick—  
Where the whirls were wild and the storm was thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
And looked below the broken tide,  
  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe,  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
  
The face that I see is graver grown,  
  
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone,  
  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I like to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray threw  
Ere the blossoms moved or the green grass grew  
O’er the moldering stern of the old canoe.  
—
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://iiif.archive.org/image/iiif/2/sim_the-womans-journal_1879-04-05_10_14%2Fsim_the-womans-journal_1879-04-05_10_14_jp2.zip%2Fsim_the-womans-journal_1879-04-05_10_14_jp2%2Fsim_the-womans-journal_1879-04-05_10_14_0001.jp2/pct:9.916534,8.402613,15.957870,29.735748/,600/0/default.jpg"/>
</td>
</tr></table>

## [From _Forest and Stream; A Journal of Outdoor Life, Travel, Nature Study, Shooting, Fishing, Yachting (1873-1930)_](https://archive.org/details/sim_forest-and-stream-a-journal-of-outdoor-life_1879-05-15_12_15/page/n0/mode/1up?view=theater)

#### 1879-05-15 &middot; [New York](http://dbpedia.org/resource/New_York_City)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

, the road under Carpenter’s Bridge, and forms a mini-  
ature lake, and it lay stretched out beneath the cloudless sky,  
pure and unbroken as a mirror, with its wooded shores gleam-  
ing aloft in unnumbered hues and unrivalled radiance, which  
autumn nowhere sbeds with so lavish a hand as in an Amer-  
ican woods, and sleeping below, reflected to the smallest leaf  
on the calm surface of the water. Attached to the bridge by  
a long chain was an old waterlogged canoe, which had once  
been used as a fishing boat, but now it was almost ready to  
fall to pieces from old age :—  
“ The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots slowly away in its living grave ;  
  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
  
Hiding the mouldering dust away. .  
  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower ;  
  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
  
Springs o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
“ The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will ;  
And lazily, in and out again,  
  
It floats tne length of the ruaty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime.  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.”  
  
The air was breathless, yet so pure and fresh that the brisk-  
est breeze that ever fanned the ocean came not more grate-  
fully to the cheek and brow than that delicious calm in which  
the mos
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">

## [From _Memphis daily appeal._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn83045160/1883-05-13/ed-1/?sp=2)

#### 1883-05-13 &middot; [Memphis, Tenn.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Memphis%2C_Tennessee)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

.  
The Federal Court is grinding along  
rapidly. The Supreme Court has had up the  
docket of the Northern District this week,  
and there are many prominent lawyers in the  
city.WILSOnA.  
THE OLN CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride  
Looks gloomity over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank  
Whore the shadow is heavy the whole ay through,  
tse at its moorines an old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
lilke the sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm ha  
At  
And crossed  
nd crossed on the railing one o&#x27;er one,  
tke the folded hands when the work is  
hile busy, back and farth between,  
Like  
done;  
wie puny, bac an tarth between,  
The spider stitches his silvesy screen  
And the solemn owl, with his all &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Settles on the side of the old canoe  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay  
Hiding the moldering dust away  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
or the ivy that manties the inliins towel,  
While many blossoms of the loveliest hue  
spring up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dull and still,  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And laztly in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain;  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
By the dipping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddied it down where the stream run  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see is the graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a silvery tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that sped,  
As I rocked where the waves their white spray  
shed,  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
ALBERI EIN

It floats the length of its rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime.  
  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
  
By the dripping bows of the old canoe.  
  
Oh, many a time with careless hand.  
  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand.  
  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
  
quick.  
  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
  
thick.  
  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
  
And looked below in the broken tide  
To see that the faces and boats were two.  
  
that were miriowed back from the old canoe.  
  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er tho crumbling side.  
And look below in tho sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown.  
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone.  
  
And tbo hands that lent to the light skiff  
  
wings  
  
Have grown familiar with sterner things ;

THE 01.1) CAXOE.  
Where the rocks are ttray, and the shore is steep.  
And the waters below iook dark and deep.  
Where the ruiop and pine, in its lonely pride.  
I.eans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rashes are long and l&lt;uik.  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank:  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
through.  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped.  
I.ike a sea bird’s wings that the storm has  
lopped.  
And crossed on the railing one o er one,  
I.ike the fold&#x27;d hands when the work is done;  
Whi’e busilv back and forth between,  
The spi h-r stretches his silvery screen.

Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stem, half sunk in the slimy wave.  
Rots slowly away in its living grave;

Hiding its laddering dust away,  
l.ik*- the hand that plants o’er a tomb a flower,  
nr the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still.  
But the twilight wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again.  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
I.ike the weary march of ttie hands of time.  
That meet and part at the noonday chime.  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew

Oh ! many a time with careless hand,

And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick.  
Where the whirls are wide and the eddies are  
thick.

And looked below in the broken tide.  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
Hut now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side.  
And look below in the sluggish tide.  
The face that 1 see there Is graver grown.  
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone.

shed.

## [From _The Washington critic._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn82000205/1886-02-18/ed-1/?sp=2)

#### 1886-02-18 &middot; [Washington, D.C.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Washington%2C_D.C.)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

d  
yet.  
THE OLD CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is  
steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
here the rugged pine, in its lovely pride,  
leans gioomlly over the murky tide,  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and  
rank  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding  
bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
through,  
There lies at its mooring the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has  
lopped,  
And crossed on the railing one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
The soldier watches his silver screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe,  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Iots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding its moldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a  
flower  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower,  
hile many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time  
That meet and part at the noonday chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
-Albert Pike, 1846.

## "The Old Canoe"

#### From _Bismarck daily tribune._

#### 1886-04-20 &middot; [Bismarck, Dakota [N.D.]](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Bismarck%2C_North_Dakota)

## [From _Middlebury register._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn93063557/1886-05-07/ed-1/?sp=2)

#### 1886-05-07 &middot; [Middlebury, Vt.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Middlebury%2C_Vermont)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
ThE OLD CANOR.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore i  
steel,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the ragged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and  
rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding  
bank,  
Where the shadows are heavy the whole day  
thotigH,  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath lop  
ped.  
nd crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silver sereen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
ots slowly away in its living grave;  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb  
flower,  
or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
prings up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light winds play with the boat at will  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wide and the eddys are  
thick.  
And longed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were fico  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone  
And the hands that lent to the light skinf  
wings  
Have grown familiar with stemer things,  
But I love to think of the houis that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls teir white spray  
threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green moss  
erew.  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe  
EMIlY R. Pag Braord, YT.

## [From _Current Literature (1888-1912)_](https://archive.org/details/sim_current-opinion_1889-03_2_3/page/n49/mode/1up?view=theater)

#### 1889-03-01 &middot; [New York](http://dbpedia.org/resource/New_York_City)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
  
  
The Old Canoe—Emily Rebecca Page  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide ;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lies at the moorings the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
  
Like a sea-bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
  
Like the folded hands when the work is done,  
While busily back and forth between,  
  
The spider stretches his silvery screen ;  
  
And the solemn owl, with his dull “ too-hoo,”  
Settles down on the sides of the old canoe.  
  
The stern half-sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots slowly in its living grave,  
  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower ;  
  
While many a blossom of the loveliest hue  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noon-tide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
  
Many a time, with careless hand  
  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick—  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick—  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things ;  
  
But I love to think of the hours that flew  
  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
  
The Legend of St. Vitus—Walter Thornbury  
  
To Cairo city, one hot afternoon,  
  
In th
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://iiif.archive.org/image/iiif/2/sim_current-opinion_1889-03_2_3%2Fsim_current-opinion_1889-03_2_3_jp2.zip%2Fsim_current-opinion_1889-03_2_3_jp2%2Fsim_current-opinion_1889-03_2_3_0049.jp2/pct:19.776570,12.335382,32.185990,64.310799/,600/0/default.jpg"/>

But, now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in thesluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
All the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff  
  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
But I love to shink of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white  
  
spray shed  
  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass  
  
O’er the moldering stern of the old canoe.

## [From _The Louisiana Democrat._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn82003389/1889-08-14/ed-1/?sp=4)

#### 1889-08-14 &middot; [Alexandria, La.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Alexandria%2C_Louisiana)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

ANA.  
  
THE OLD CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is  
steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, In its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding  
bank;  
Where the shadow Is heavy the whole day  
through.  
There lies at its mooring the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has  
lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done,  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl with the dull &quot;too-whoe,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the glimy wave,  
Rots slowly away on its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its null decay,  
Hiding its mouldering days away.  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a  
flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
The twilivht wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part with the mountain chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the drippling bough of the old canoe.  
Oh! many a time with ceaseless hand  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand.  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick,  
And laughed as I learned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the laces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff  
wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white  
spray shed  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass  
grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
-Albert Pike, in Baltimorean.  
HEZIKIAR AN&#x27; ME.  
Their Tour to the City-Adventures  
of the Trip.  
CHAPTER I.  
IN TH
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">

THE OLD CANOC.

## [From _Good Housekeeping 1885-2015_](https://archive.org/details/sim_good-housekeeping_good-housekeeping_1889-09-14_9_10/page/n23/mode/1up?view=theater)

s Companion.  
  
THE OLD CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank ;  
  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
through,  
There lies at its mooring the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
  
Like a sea bird’s wings that the storm has  
lopped,  
  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
  
Like the folded hands when the work is done,  
  
While busily, back and forth between,  
  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
  
And the solemn owl with the dull ‘‘ too-whoo,”  
  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding its moldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower,  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
  
But the twilight wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
  
Oh! many a time with careless hand,  
  
I have pushed it away from the pelly strand,  
  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,  
  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick,  
  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
  
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,  
  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
  
shed,  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass grew  
O’er the moldering stern of the old canoe.

## [From _St. Tammany farmer._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn82015387/1889-09-28/ed-1/?sp=4)

#### 1889-09-28 &middot; [Covington, La.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Covington%2C_Louisiana)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

.  
covinGToN. LOUISIANA,  
THE OLD CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is  
steep  
Aad the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank.  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding  
bank:  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
through.  
There lies at its mooring the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hat  
pped  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done,  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl with the dull &quot;too-whoc.&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the glimy wave,  
Rots slowly away on its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding its mouldering days away.  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a  
fower.  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower:  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
The twilight wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part with the mountain chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the drippling bough of the old canoe.  
Oh! many a time with ceaseless hand  
have pushed it away from the pebbly strand.  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,  
 Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick,  
And laughed as I learned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff  
wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white  
spray shed  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass  
grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://tile.loc.gov/image-services/iiif/service:ndnp:lu:batch_lu_hayes_ver01:data:sn82015387:00202190443:1889092801:0586/pct:5.476974,7.259615,13.930921,27.920673/!600,600/0/default.jpg"/>

|  
  
THE OLD CANOE.  
By ALBERT PIKE,  
| WHERE the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
  
| And the waters below look dark and deep;  
  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
There lies at its mooring the old canoe,  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
  
Like a sea-bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
  
Like the folded hands when the work is done,  
While busily back and forth between  
  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
  
And the solemn owl with the dull “ too-whoo,”  
Sottles down on the side of the old canoe,  
  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding its moldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe,  
  
  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
  
Lut the twilight wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe,

wings

But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white  
spray shed,  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass  
grow  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

## [From _Middlebury register._](https://www.loc.gov/resource/sn93063557/1896-11-06/ed-1/?sp=1)

#### 1896-11-06 &middot; [Middlebury, Vt.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Middlebury%2C_Vermont)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
ThE OLD CANOE.  
By Emily R. Page of Bradford.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is  
steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the ragged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding  
Dalk;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
tttsts  
Lays at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath  
lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solenin owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave;  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Biding the mouldering dust away;  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a  
flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light winds play with the boat at will;  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That mneet and part at the noontide chime;  
nd the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time, with a careless hand,  
liatve bislied it away from the pebbly strand,  
ht Dattt led it Gowh where the Streain runs  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick.  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
aht looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were too  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
tiae whown tamilial with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whiris their white spray  
threw,  
Ere the blossons waved, or the green moss  
grew,  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
They are s

## [From _Current Literature (1888-1912)_](https://archive.org/details/sim_current-opinion_1897-05_21_5/page/n32/mode/1up?view=theater)

#### 1897-05-01 &middot; [New York](http://dbpedia.org/resource/New_York_City)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
  
Like a sea-bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
  
And the solemn owl, with his dull “too hoo,”  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
  
And the green moss creeps o’er the dull decay  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wide and the eddies thick,  
\nd laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide  
  
To see that the faces and boat were two  
  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
  
But now as I lean o’er the crumbling side  
  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
  
But I love to think of the hours sped  
  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray shed,  
Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew  
O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
  
o
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://iiif.archive.org/image/iiif/2/sim_current-opinion_1897-05_21_5%2Fsim_current-opinion_1897-05_21_5_jp2.zip%2Fsim_current-opinion_1897-05_21_5_jp2%2Fsim_current-opinion_1897-05_21_5_0032.jp2/pct:48.746944,11.578947,34.229829,62.741228/,600/0/default.jpg"/>

Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide,  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
  
through,  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped.  
Like seabird’s wings that the storms had lopped,  
And crossed on the railing one o’er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done,  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull “too-hoo,”  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
Th« stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding its mol’dring dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the lengths of the rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
*By the rippling bow of the old canoe.  
  
•Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
T have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wide and the eddies are  
  
thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocky side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That where mirrored back from the old canoe,  
  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lend to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
  
shed,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
•O’er the moldering stern of the old canoe.

The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like seabird’s wings that storms had  
lopped,  
And cross,d on the railing one o’er one  
Like the folded hands when the work is  
done,  
While busily back and forth between  
Ttie spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And tho solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too­  
hoo’’  
Settles down on the s^de of the old canoe.  
The stern half-sunk in the slimy wave  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er Its dull de­  
cay,  
Hiding its moulding dust away.  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a  
flower  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still  
But the light wind plays with the boat at  
’ will.  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet end part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turning  
anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe. ^  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
1 have pushed it away from the pebbly  
strand,  
And paddled R down where the stream  
runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies  
are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking  
side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To sec that the faces and boats were two.  
That were mirrored back from the old ca­  
noe.  
But now. as 1 lean o’er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown.  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer  
tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff  
wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to&#x27; think of the hours that sped  
As I rooked where the whirls their white  
spray shed.  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass  
grew  
O&#x27;er tho coldlng stern of the old canoe.
